<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For the Greek language used during particular eras, see <a href="/wiki/Proto-Greek_language" title="Proto-Greek language">Proto-Greek language</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mycenaean_Greek" title="Mycenaean Greek">Mycenaean Greek</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek" title="Ancient Greek">Ancient Greek</a>, <a href="/wiki/Koine_Greek" title="Koine Greek">Koine Greek</a>, <a href="/wiki/Medieval_Greek" title="Medieval Greek">Medieval Greek</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Modern_Greek" title="Modern Greek">Modern Greek</a>.</div>

For an introductory guide on IPA symbols, see <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA" title="Help:IPA">Help:IPA</a>.</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Greek</b> (<a href="/wiki/Modern_Greek" title="Modern Greek">Modern Greek</a>: <span lang="el">Ελληνικά</span>, <small><a href="/wiki/Romanization_of_Greek" title="Romanization of Greek">romanized</a>: </small><span title="Greek-language romanization"><i lang="el-Latn">Elliniká</i></span>; <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_language" title="Ancient Greek language">Ancient Greek</a>: <span lang="grc">Ἑλληνική</span>, <small><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Romanization_of_Ancient_Greek" title="Romanization of Ancient Greek">romanized</a>: </small><span title="Ancient Greek-language romanization"><i lang="grc-Latn">Hellēnikḗ</i></span>) is an independent branch of the <a href="/wiki/Indo-European_languages" title="Indo-European languages">Indo-European</a> family of languages, native to <a href="/wiki/Greece" title="Greece">Greece</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cyprus" title="Cyprus">Cyprus</a>, southern <a href="/wiki/Albania" title="Albania">Albania</a>, and other regions of the <a href="/wiki/Balkans" title="Balkans">Balkans</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Black_Sea" title="Black Sea">Black Sea</a> coast, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asia_Minor" title="Asia Minor">Asia Minor</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Mediterranean" title="Eastern Mediterranean">Eastern Mediterranean</a>. It has the <a href="/wiki/List_of_languages_by_first_written_accounts" title="List of languages by first written accounts">longest documented history</a> of any Indo-European language, spanning at least 3,400 years of written records.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-2">[2]</a></sup> Its writing system is the <a href="/wiki/Greek_alphabet" title="Greek alphabet">Greek alphabet</a>, which has been used for approximately 2,800 years;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> previously, Greek was recorded in writing systems such as <a href="/wiki/Linear_B" title="Linear B">Linear B</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Cypriot_syllabary" title="Cypriot syllabary">Cypriot syllabary</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> The alphabet arose from the <a href="/wiki/Phoenician_alphabet" title="Phoenician alphabet">Phoenician script</a> and was in turn the basis of the <a href="/wiki/Latin_script" title="Latin script">Latin</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cyrillic" title="Cyrillic">Cyrillic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Armenian_alphabet" title="Armenian alphabet">Armenian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Coptic_alphabet" title="Coptic alphabet">Coptic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gothic_alphabet" title="Gothic alphabet">Gothic</a>, and many other writing systems.
</p><p>The Greek language holds a very important place in the history of the <a href="/wiki/Western_world" title="Western world">Western world</a>. Beginning with the epics of <a href="/wiki/Homer" title="Homer">Homer</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_literature" title="Ancient Greek literature">ancient Greek literature</a> includes many works of lasting importance in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/European_canon" title="European canon">European canon</a>. Greek is also the language in which many of the foundational texts in science and philosophy were originally composed. The <a href="/wiki/New_Testament" title="New Testament">New Testament</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Bible" title="Bible">Christian Bible</a> was also originally written in Greek.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> Together with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latin_language" title="Latin language">Latin</a> texts and traditions of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_world" title="Roman world">Roman world</a>, the Greek texts and Greek societies of antiquity constitute the objects of study of the discipline of <a href="/wiki/Classics" title="Classics">Classics</a>.
</p><p>During <a href="/wiki/Classical_antiquity" title="Classical antiquity">antiquity</a>, Greek was by far the most widely spoken <a href="/wiki/Lingua_franca" title="Lingua franca">lingua franca</a> in the Mediterranean world. It eventually became the official language of the <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">Byzantine Empire</a> and developed into <a href="/wiki/Medieval_Greek" title="Medieval Greek">Medieval Greek</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> In its <a href="/wiki/Modern_Greek" title="Modern Greek">modern form</a>, Greek is the official language of Greece and Cyprus and one of the 24 <a href="/wiki/Languages_of_the_European_Union#Official_EU_languages" title="Languages of the European Union">official languages of the European Union</a>. It is spoken by at least 13.5 million people today in Greece, Cyprus, <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a>, Albania, <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a>, and the many other countries of the <a href="/wiki/Greek_diaspora" title="Greek diaspora">Greek diaspora</a>.
</p><p>Greek <a href="/wiki/Root_(linguistics)" title="Root (linguistics)">roots</a> have been widely used for centuries and continue to be widely used to coin new words in other languages; <a href="/wiki/List_of_Greek_and_Latin_roots_in_English" title="List of Greek and Latin roots in English">Greek and Latin</a> are the predominant sources of <a href="/wiki/International_scientific_vocabulary" title="International scientific vocabulary">international scientific vocabulary</a>.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Homer_British_Museum.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="635" decoding="async" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Homer_British_Museum.jpg/180px-Homer_British_Museum.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Homer_British_Museum.jpg/270px-Homer_British_Museum.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Homer_British_Museum.jpg/360px-Homer_British_Museum.jpg 2x" width="180"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Homer_British_Museum.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Idealised portrayal of the author <a href="/wiki/Homer" title="Homer">Homer</a></div></div></div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Greek_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Greek" title="History of Greek">History of Greek</a></div>
<p>Greek has been spoken in the <a href="/wiki/Balkans" title="Balkans">Balkan peninsula</a> since around the 3rd millennium BC,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BVG_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-BVG-9">[9]</a></sup> or possibly earlier.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> The earliest written evidence is a <a href="/wiki/Linear_B" title="Linear B">Linear B</a> <a href="/wiki/Clay_tablet" title="Clay tablet">clay tablet</a> found in <a href="/wiki/Messenia" title="Messenia">Messenia</a> that dates to between 1450 and 1350 BC,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> making Greek the world's <a href="/wiki/List_of_languages_by_first_written_accounts" title="List of languages by first written accounts">oldest recorded</a> <a href="/wiki/Modern_language" title="Modern language">living language</a>. Among the Indo-European languages, its date of earliest written attestation is matched only by the now-extinct <a href="/wiki/Anatolian_languages" title="Anatolian languages">Anatolian languages</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Periods">Periods</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Greek_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Periods">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Proto_Greek_Area_reconstruction.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="380" data-file-width="460" decoding="async" height="182" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Proto_Greek_Area_reconstruction.png/220px-Proto_Greek_Area_reconstruction.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Proto_Greek_Area_reconstruction.png/330px-Proto_Greek_Area_reconstruction.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Proto_Greek_Area_reconstruction.png/440px-Proto_Greek_Area_reconstruction.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Proto_Greek_Area_reconstruction.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Proto-Greek_language" title="Proto-Greek language">Proto-Greek</a>-speaking area according to linguist <a href="/wiki/Vladimir_I._Georgiev" title="Vladimir I. Georgiev">Vladimir I. Georgiev</a></div></div></div>
<p>The Greek language is conventionally divided into the following periods:
</p>
<ul><li><b><a href="/wiki/Proto-Greek_language" title="Proto-Greek language">Proto-Greek</a></b>: the unrecorded but assumed last ancestor of all known varieties of Greek. The unity of Proto-Greek would have ended as Hellenic migrants entered the <a href="/wiki/Geography_of_Greece" title="Geography of Greece">Greek peninsula</a> sometime in the <a href="/wiki/Neolithic" title="Neolithic">Neolithic</a> era or the <a href="/wiki/Bronze_Age" title="Bronze Age">Bronze Age</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[note 1]</a></sup></li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Mycenaean_Greek" title="Mycenaean Greek">Mycenaean Greek</a></b>: the language of the <a href="/wiki/Mycenaean_Greece" title="Mycenaean Greece">Mycenaean civilization</a>. It is recorded in the <a href="/wiki/Linear_B" title="Linear B">Linear B</a> script on tablets dating from the 15th century BC onwards.</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek" title="Ancient Greek">Ancient Greek</a></b>: in its various <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_dialects" title="Ancient Greek dialects">dialects</a>, the language of the <a href="/wiki/Archaic_Greece" title="Archaic Greece">Archaic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Classical_Greece" title="Classical Greece">Classical</a> periods of the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">ancient Greek civilization</a>. It was widely known throughout the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a>. Ancient Greek fell into disuse in western Europe in the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a>, but remained officially in use in the <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">Byzantine</a> world and was reintroduced to the rest of Europe with the <a href="/wiki/Fall_of_Constantinople" title="Fall of Constantinople">Fall of Constantinople</a> and <a href="/wiki/Greeks" title="Greeks">Greek</a> migration to western Europe.</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Koine_Greek" title="Koine Greek">Koine Greek</a></b> (also known as <b>Hellenistic Greek</b>): The fusion of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ionian_Greek" title="Ionian Greek">Ionian</a> with <a href="/wiki/Attic_Greek" title="Attic Greek">Attic</a>, the dialect of <a href="/wiki/Classical_Athens" title="Classical Athens">Athens</a>, began the process that resulted in the creation of the first common Greek dialect, which became a <a href="/wiki/Lingua_franca" title="Lingua franca">lingua franca</a> across the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Mediterranean" title="Eastern Mediterranean">Eastern Mediterranean</a> and <a href="/wiki/Near_East" title="Near East">Near East</a>. Koine Greek can be initially traced within the armies and conquered territories of <a href="/wiki/Alexander_the_Great" title="Alexander the Great">Alexander the Great</a>; after the Hellenistic colonization of the known world, it was spoken from <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a> to the fringes of <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>. After the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Republic" title="Roman Republic">Roman</a> conquest of Greece, an unofficial <a href="/wiki/Multilingualism" title="Multilingualism">bilingualism</a> of Greek and <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> was established in the city of <a href="/wiki/Rome" title="Rome">Rome</a> and Koine Greek became a first or second language in the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a>. The origin of <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christianity</a> can also be traced through Koine Greek because the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Twelve_Apostles" title="Twelve Apostles">Apostles</a> used this form of the language to spread Christianity. Because it was the original language of the <a href="/wiki/New_Testament" title="New Testament">New Testament</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Old_Testament" title="Old Testament">Old Testament</a> was translated into it as the <a href="/wiki/Septuagint" title="Septuagint">Septuagint</a>, that variety of Koine Greek may be referred to as <b>New Testament Greek</b> or sometimes <b>Biblical Greek</b>.</li></ul>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Anatolian_Greek_dialects.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="268" data-file-width="399" decoding="async" height="148" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Anatolian_Greek_dialects.png/220px-Anatolian_Greek_dialects.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Anatolian_Greek_dialects.png/330px-Anatolian_Greek_dialects.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/96/Anatolian_Greek_dialects.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Anatolian_Greek_dialects.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Distribution of varieties of Greek in <a href="/wiki/Anatolia" title="Anatolia">Anatolia</a>, 1910. <a href="/wiki/Modern_Greek#Demotic" title="Modern Greek">Demotic</a> in yellow. <a href="/wiki/Pontic_Greek" title="Pontic Greek">Pontic</a> in orange. <a href="/wiki/Cappadocian_Greek" title="Cappadocian Greek">Cappadocian Greek</a> in green, with green dots indicating individual Cappadocian Greek villages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[12]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<ul><li><b><a href="/wiki/Medieval_Greek" title="Medieval Greek">Medieval Greek</a></b> (also known as <b>Byzantine Greek</b>): the continuation of Koine Greek up to the demise of the <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">Byzantine Empire</a> in the 15th century. <i>Medieval Greek</i> is a cover phrase for a whole continuum of different speech and writing styles, ranging from vernacular continuations of spoken Koine that were already approaching Modern Greek in many respects, to highly learned forms imitating classical Attic. Much of the written Greek that was used as the official language of the Byzantine Empire was an eclectic middle-ground variety based on the tradition of written Koine.</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Modern_Greek" title="Modern Greek">Modern Greek</a></b> (also known as <b>Neo-Hellenic</b>):<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ethnologue_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ethnologue-14">[13]</a></sup> Stemming from Medieval Greek, Modern Greek usages can be traced in the Byzantine period, as early as the 11th century. It is the language used by the modern Greeks, and, apart from Standard Modern Greek, there are several <a href="/wiki/Varieties_of_Modern_Greek" title="Varieties of Modern Greek">dialects</a> of it.</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Diglossia">Diglossia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Greek_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Diglossia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Greek_language_question" title="Greek language question">Greek language question</a></div>
<p>In the modern era, the Greek language entered a state of <a href="/wiki/Diglossia" title="Diglossia">diglossia</a>: the coexistence of vernacular and archaizing written forms of the language. What came to be known as the <a href="/wiki/Greek_language_question" title="Greek language question">Greek language question</a> was a polarization between two competing varieties of Modern Greek: <a href="/wiki/Demotic_Greek" title="Demotic Greek">Dimotiki</a>, the vernacular form of Modern Greek proper, and <a href="/wiki/Katharevousa" title="Katharevousa">Katharevousa</a>, meaning 'purified', a compromise between Dimotiki and <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek" title="Ancient Greek">Ancient Greek</a> developed in the early 19th century that was used for literary and official purposes in the newly formed Greek state. In 1976, Dimotiki was declared the official language of Greece, after having incorporated features of Katharevousa and thus giving birth to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Standard_Modern_Greek" title="Standard Modern Greek">Standard Modern Greek</a>, used today for all official purposes and in <a href="/wiki/Education_in_Greece" title="Education in Greece">education</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[14]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Historical_unity">Historical unity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Greek_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Historical unity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Modern_Greek_dialects_en.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="700" data-file-width="750" decoding="async" height="224" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Modern_Greek_dialects_en.svg/240px-Modern_Greek_dialects_en.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Modern_Greek_dialects_en.svg/360px-Modern_Greek_dialects_en.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Modern_Greek_dialects_en.svg/480px-Modern_Greek_dialects_en.svg.png 2x" width="240"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Modern_Greek_dialects_en.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The distribution of major modern Greek dialect areas</div></div></div>
<p>The historical unity and continuing identity between the various stages of the Greek language are often emphasized. Although Greek has undergone morphological and phonological changes comparable to those seen in other languages, never since classical antiquity has its cultural, literary, and orthographic tradition been interrupted to the extent that one can speak of a new language emerging. Greek speakers today still tend to regard literary works of ancient Greek as part of their own rather than a foreign language.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[15]</a></sup> It is also often stated that the historical changes have been relatively slight compared with some other languages. According to one estimation, "<a href="/wiki/Homeric_Greek" title="Homeric Greek">Homeric Greek</a> is probably closer to <a href="/wiki/Demotic_Greek" title="Demotic Greek">Demotic</a> than 12-century <a href="/wiki/Middle_English" title="Middle English">Middle English</a> is to <a href="/wiki/Modern_English" title="Modern English">modern spoken English</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[16]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geographic_distribution">Geographic distribution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Greek_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Geographic distribution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Greeks" title="Greeks">Greeks</a> and <a href="/wiki/Greek_diaspora" title="Greek diaspora">Greek diaspora</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Greek_language_in_the_Russian_Empire_(1897).svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2744" data-file-width="4648" decoding="async" height="130" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Greek_language_in_the_Russian_Empire_%281897%29.svg/220px-Greek_language_in_the_Russian_Empire_%281897%29.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Greek_language_in_the_Russian_Empire_%281897%29.svg/330px-Greek_language_in_the_Russian_Empire_%281897%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Greek_language_in_the_Russian_Empire_%281897%29.svg/440px-Greek_language_in_the_Russian_Empire_%281897%29.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Greek_language_in_the_Russian_Empire_(1897).svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Geographic distribution of Greek language in the Russian Empire (1897 census)</div></div></div>
<p>Greek is spoken today by at least 13 million people, principally in Greece and Cyprus along with a sizable Greek-speaking minority in <a href="/wiki/Greeks_in_Albania" title="Greeks in Albania">Albania</a> near the Greek-Albanian border.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ethnologue_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ethnologue-14">[13]</a></sup> A significant percentage of Albania's population has some basic knowledge of the Greek language due in part to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Albanian_immigrants_in_Greece" title="Albanian immigrants in Greece">Albanian wave of immigration to Greece</a> in the 1980s and '90s. Prior to the <a href="/wiki/Greco-Turkish_War_(1919%E2%80%931922)" title="Greco-Turkish War (1919–1922)">Greco-Turkish War</a> and the resulting <a href="/wiki/Population_exchange_between_Greece_and_Turkey" title="Population exchange between Greece and Turkey">population exchange in 1923</a> a very large population of Greek-speakers also existed in <a href="/wiki/Greeks_in_Turkey" title="Greeks in Turkey">Turkey</a>, though very few remain today.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-:1-2">[2]</a></sup> A small Greek-speaking community is also found in <a href="/wiki/Greeks_in_Bulgaria" title="Greeks in Bulgaria">Bulgaria</a> near the Greek-Bulgarian border. Greek is also spoken worldwide by the sizable <a href="/wiki/Greek_diaspora" title="Greek diaspora">Greek diaspora</a> which has notable communities in the <a href="/wiki/Greek_Americans" title="Greek Americans">United States</a>, <a href="/wiki/Greek_Australians" title="Greek Australians">Australia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Greek_Canadians" title="Greek Canadians">Canada</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greek_South_Africans" title="Greek South Africans">South Africa</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greek_Chilean" title="Greek Chilean">Chile</a>, <a href="/wiki/Greek_Brazilians" title="Greek Brazilians">Brazil</a>, <a href="/wiki/Greek_Argentines" title="Greek Argentines">Argentina</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greeks_in_Russia_and_the_Soviet_Union" title="Greeks in Russia and the Soviet Union">Russia</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greeks_in_Ukraine" title="Greeks in Ukraine">Ukraine</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Greeks_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Greeks in the United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a>, and throughout the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a>, especially in <a href="/wiki/Greeks_in_Germany" title="Greeks in Germany">Germany</a>.
</p><p>Historically, significant Greek-speaking communities and regions were found throughout the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Mediterranean" title="Eastern Mediterranean">Eastern Mediterranean</a>, in what are today <a href="/wiki/Southern_Italy" title="Southern Italy">Southern Italy</a>, Turkey, Cyprus, <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">Syria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lebanon" title="Lebanon">Lebanon</a>, <a href="/wiki/State_of_Palestine" title="State of Palestine">Palestine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">Israel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Libya" title="Libya">Libya</a>; in the area of the <a href="/wiki/Black_Sea" title="Black Sea">Black Sea</a>, in what are today Turkey, <a href="/wiki/Bulgaria" title="Bulgaria">Bulgaria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Romania" title="Romania">Romania</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ukraine" title="Ukraine">Ukraine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Georgia_(country)" title="Georgia (country)">Georgia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Armenia" title="Armenia">Armenia</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Azerbaijan" title="Azerbaijan">Azerbaijan</a>; and, to a lesser extent, in the Western <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_Sea" title="Mediterranean Sea">Mediterranean</a> in and around <a href="/wiki/Greek_colonisation" title="Greek colonisation">colonies</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Massalia" title="Massalia">Massalia</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Monoikos" title="Monoikos">Monoikos</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Mainake_(Greek_settlement)" title="Mainake (Greek settlement)">Mainake</a>. It was also used as a liturgical language in Christian Nubian kingdom of <a href="/wiki/Makuria" title="Makuria">Makuria</a> which was in modern day <a href="/wiki/Sudan" title="Sudan">Sudan</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWelsby2002239_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWelsby2002239-18">[17]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Official_status">Official status</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Greek_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Official status">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Greek, in its modern form, is the <a href="/wiki/Official_language" title="Official language">official language</a> of Greece, where it is spoken by almost the entire population.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[18]</a></sup> It is also the official language of Cyprus (nominally alongside <a href="/wiki/Turkish_language" title="Turkish language">Turkish</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GreekCyprus_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-GreekCyprus-20">[19]</a></sup> Because of the membership of Greece and Cyprus in the European Union, Greek is one of the organization's <a href="/wiki/Languages_of_the_European_Union#Official_EU_languages" title="Languages of the European Union">24 official languages</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-European_Union_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-European_Union-21">[20]</a></sup> Greek is recognized as a <a href="/wiki/Minority_language" title="Minority language">minority language</a> in Albania and used co-officially in some of the municipalities in <a href="/wiki/Gjirokast%C3%ABr_District" title="Gjirokastër District">Gjirokastër</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sarand%C3%AB_District" title="Sarandë District">Sarandë</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[21]</a></sup> It is also an official minority language in the regions of <a href="/wiki/Apulia" title="Apulia">Apulia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Calabria" title="Calabria">Calabria</a> in Italy. In the framework of the <a href="/wiki/European_Charter_for_Regional_or_Minority_Languages" title="European Charter for Regional or Minority Languages">European Charter for Regional or Minority Languages</a>, Greek is protected and promoted officially as a regional and minority language in Armenia, <a href="/wiki/Hungary" title="Hungary">Hungary</a>, Romania, and Ukraine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CouncilofEurope_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-CouncilofEurope-23">[22]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Characteristics">Characteristics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Greek_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Characteristics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_grammar" title="Ancient Greek grammar">Ancient Greek grammar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Koine_Greek_grammar" title="Koine Greek grammar">Koine Greek grammar</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Modern_Greek_grammar" title="Modern Greek grammar">Modern Greek grammar</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Phonology" title="Phonology">phonology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Morphology_(linguistics)" title="Morphology (linguistics)">morphology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Syntax" title="Syntax">syntax</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Vocabulary" title="Vocabulary">vocabulary</a> of the language show both conservative and innovative tendencies across the entire attestation of the language from the ancient to the modern period. The division into conventional periods is, as with all such periodizations, relatively arbitrary, especially because, in all periods, Ancient Greek has enjoyed high prestige, and the literate borrowed heavily from it.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Phonology">Phonology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Greek_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Phonology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Modern_Greek_phonology" title="Modern Greek phonology">Modern Greek phonology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Koine_Greek_phonology" title="Koine Greek phonology">Koine Greek phonology</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_phonology" title="Ancient Greek phonology">Ancient Greek phonology</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><audio class="thumbimage" controls="" data-durationhint="23" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons" data-mwtitle="Seferisrede.ogg" id="mwe_player_0" preload="none" style="width:220px;" width="220"><source data-bandwidth="66224" data-height="0" data-shorttitle="Ogg source" data-title="Original Ogg file (66 kbps)" data-width="0" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/56/Seferisrede.ogg" type='audio/ogg; codecs="vorbis"'/><source data-bandwidth="118880" data-height="0" data-shorttitle="MP3" data-title="MP3" data-transcodekey="mp3" data-width="0" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/5/56/Seferisrede.ogg/Seferisrede.ogg.mp3" type="audio/mpeg"/></audio> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Seferisrede.ogg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Spoken <a href="/wiki/Modern_Greek" title="Modern Greek">Modern Greek</a></div></div></div>
<p>Across its history, the syllabic structure of Greek has varied little: Greek shows a mixed syllable structure, permitting complex syllabic onsets but very restricted codas. It has only <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oral_vowels" title="Oral vowels">oral vowels</a> and a fairly stable set of consonantal <a href="/wiki/Phonemic_contrast" title="Phonemic contrast">contrasts</a>. The main phonological changes occurred during the Hellenistic and Roman period (see <a href="/wiki/Koine_Greek_phonology" title="Koine Greek phonology">Koine Greek phonology</a> for details):
</p>
<ul><li>replacement of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pitch_accent" title="Pitch accent">pitch accent</a> with a <a href="/wiki/Stress_(linguistics)" title="Stress (linguistics)">stress accent</a>.</li>
<li>simplification of the system of <a href="/wiki/Vowel" title="Vowel">vowels</a> and <a href="/wiki/Diphthong" title="Diphthong">diphthongs</a>: loss of vowel length distinction, monophthongisation of most diphthongs and several steps in a <a href="/wiki/Chain_shift" title="Chain shift">chain shift</a> of vowels towards <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/i/</span> (<a href="/wiki/Iotacism" title="Iotacism">iotacism</a>).</li>
<li>development of the <a href="/wiki/Voicelessness" title="Voicelessness">voiceless</a> <a href="/wiki/Aspirated_consonant" title="Aspirated consonant">aspirated</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stop_consonant" title="Stop consonant">plosives</a> <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/pʰ/</span> and <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/tʰ/</span> to the voiceless <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fricative_consonant" title="Fricative consonant">fricatives</a> <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/f/</span> and <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/θ/</span>, respectively; the similar development of <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/kʰ/</span> to <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/x/</span> may have taken place later (the phonological changes are not reflected in the orthography, and both earlier and later phonemes are written with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phi_(letter)" title="Phi (letter)">φ</a>, <a href="/wiki/Theta" title="Theta">θ</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Chi_(letter)" title="Chi (letter)">χ</a>).</li>
<li>development of the <a href="/wiki/Voice_(phonetics)" title="Voice (phonetics)">voiced</a> plosives <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/b/</span>, <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/d/</span>, and <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ɡ/</span> to their voiced fricative counterparts <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/β/</span> (later <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/v/</span>), <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ð/</span>, and <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ɣ/</span>.</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Morphology">Morphology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Greek_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Morphology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In all its stages, the <a href="/wiki/Morphology_(linguistics)" title="Morphology (linguistics)">morphology</a> of Greek shows an extensive set of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Productive_(linguistics)" title="Productive (linguistics)">productive</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Derivational_affix" title="Derivational affix">derivational affixes</a>, a limited but productive system of <a href="/wiki/Compound_(linguistics)" title="Compound (linguistics)">compounding</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[23]</a></sup> and a rich <a href="/wiki/Inflection" title="Inflection">inflectional</a> system. Although its morphological categories have been fairly stable over time, morphological changes are present throughout, particularly in the <a href="/wiki/Nominal_(linguistics)" title="Nominal (linguistics)">nominal</a> and verbal systems. The major change in the nominal morphology since the classical stage was the disuse of the <a href="/wiki/Dative_case" title="Dative case">dative case</a> (its functions being largely taken over by the <a href="/wiki/Genitive_case" title="Genitive case">genitive</a>). The verbal system has lost the <a href="/wiki/Infinitive" title="Infinitive">infinitive</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Synthetic_(linguistics)" title="Synthetic (linguistics)">synthetically</a>-formed future, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Perfect_tense" title="Perfect tense">perfect tenses</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Optative_mood" title="Optative mood">optative mood</a>. Many have been replaced by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Periphrastic" title="Periphrastic">periphrastic</a> (<a href="/wiki/Analytic_language" title="Analytic language">analytical</a>) forms.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Nouns_and_adjectives">Nouns and adjectives</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Greek_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Nouns and adjectives">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Pronouns show distinctions in <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_person" title="Grammatical person">person</a> (1st, 2nd, and 3rd), <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_number" title="Grammatical number">number</a> (singular, <a href="/wiki/Dual_(grammatical_number)" title="Dual (grammatical number)">dual</a>, and plural in the ancient language; singular and plural alone in later stages), and <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_gender" title="Grammatical gender">gender</a> (masculine, feminine, and neuter), and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Declension_(linguistics)" title="Declension (linguistics)">decline</a> for <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_case" title="Grammatical case">case</a> (from six cases in the earliest forms attested to four in the modern language).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[note 2]</a></sup> Nouns, articles, and adjectives show all the distinctions except for a person. Both <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Attributive_adjective" title="Attributive adjective">attributive</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Predicative_adjective" title="Predicative adjective">predicative</a> adjectives <a href="/wiki/Agreement_(linguistics)" title="Agreement (linguistics)">agree</a> with the noun.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Verbs">Verbs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Greek_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Verbs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The inflectional categories of the Greek verb have likewise remained largely the same over the course of the language's history but with significant changes in the number of distinctions within each category and their morphological expression. Greek verbs have <a href="/wiki/Synthetic_language" title="Synthetic language">synthetic</a> inflectional forms for:
</p>
<table class="wikitable">

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Syntax">Syntax</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Greek_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Syntax">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Many aspects of the <a href="/wiki/Syntax" title="Syntax">syntax</a> of Greek have remained constant: verbs agree with their subject only, the use of the surviving cases is largely intact (nominative for subjects and predicates, accusative for objects of most verbs and many prepositions, genitive for possessors), articles precede nouns, adpositions are largely prepositional, relative clauses follow the noun they modify and relative pronouns are clause-initial. However, the morphological changes also have their counterparts in the syntax, and there are also significant differences between the syntax of the ancient and that of the <a href="/wiki/Modern_Greek_grammar" title="Modern Greek grammar">modern form of the language</a>. Ancient Greek made great use of participial constructions and of constructions involving the infinitive, and the modern variety lacks the infinitive entirely (employing a raft of new periphrastic constructions instead) and uses participles more restrictively. The loss of the dative led to a rise of prepositional indirect objects (and the use of the genitive to directly mark these as well). Ancient Greek tended to be verb-final, but neutral word order in the modern language is VSO or SVO.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Vocabulary">Vocabulary</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Greek_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Vocabulary">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Modern Greek inherits most of its vocabulary from Ancient Greek, which in turn is an Indo-European language, but also includes a number of <a href="/wiki/Pre-Greek_substrate" title="Pre-Greek substrate">borrowings</a> from the languages of the populations that inhabited Greece before the arrival of Proto-Greeks,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[24]</a></sup> some documented in <a href="/wiki/Linear_B" title="Linear B">Mycenaean texts</a>; they include a large number of Greek <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Toponym" title="Toponym">toponyms</a>. The form and meaning of many words have changed. <a href="/wiki/Loanword" title="Loanword">Loanwords</a> (words of foreign origin) have entered the language, mainly from Latin, <a href="/wiki/Venetian_language#History" title="Venetian language">Venetian</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Turkish_language" title="Turkish language">Turkish</a>. During the older periods of Greek, loanwords into Greek acquired Greek inflections, thus leaving only a foreign root word. Modern borrowings (from the 20th century on), especially from <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French</a> and <a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">English</a>, are typically not inflected; other modern borrowings are derived from <a href="/wiki/South_Slavic_languages" title="South Slavic languages">South Slavic</a> (<a href="/wiki/Macedonian_language" title="Macedonian language">Macedonian</a>/<a href="/wiki/Bulgarian_language" title="Bulgarian language">Bulgarian</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Romance_languages" title="Eastern Romance languages">Eastern Romance languages</a> (<a href="/wiki/Aromanian_language" title="Aromanian language">Aromanian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Megleno-Romanian_language" title="Megleno-Romanian language">Megleno-Romanian</a>).
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Greek_loanwords_in_other_languages">Greek loanwords in other languages</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Greek_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Greek loanwords in other languages">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/English_words_of_Greek_origin" title="English words of Greek origin">English words of Greek origin</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_Greek_and_Latin_roots_in_English" title="List of Greek and Latin roots in English">List of Greek and Latin roots in English</a></div>
<p>Greek words have been widely borrowed into other languages, including English. Example words include: <i><a href="/wiki/Mathematics" title="Mathematics">mathematics</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Physics" title="Physics">physics</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Astronomy" title="Astronomy">astronomy</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Democracy" title="Democracy">democracy</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">philosophy</a></i>, <i><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/athletics" title="wikt:athletics">athletics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Theatre" title="Theatre">theatre</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rhetoric" title="Rhetoric">rhetoric</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Baptism" title="Baptism">baptism</a></i>, <i><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/evangelist" title="wikt:evangelist">evangelist</a></i>, etc. Moreover, Greek words and <a href="/wiki/Morpheme" title="Morpheme">word elements</a> continue to be productive as a basis for coinages: <i><a href="/wiki/Anthropology" title="Anthropology">anthropology</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Photography" title="Photography">photography</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Telephony" title="Telephony">telephony</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Isomer" title="Isomer">isomer</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Biomechanics" title="Biomechanics">biomechanics</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Cinematography" title="Cinematography">cinematography</a></i>, etc. Together with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scientific_Latin" title="Scientific Latin">Latin words</a>, they form the <a href="/wiki/International_scientific_vocabulary" title="International scientific vocabulary">foundation of international scientific and technical vocabulary</a>; for example, all words ending in <i>–logy</i> ("discourse"). There are many <a href="/wiki/English_words_of_Greek_origin" title="English words of Greek origin">English words of Greek origin</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[26]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Classification">Classification</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Greek_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Classification">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Greek is an independent branch of the <a href="/wiki/Indo-European_languages" title="Indo-European languages">Indo-European</a> language family. The ancient language most closely related to it may be <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Macedonian_language" title="Ancient Macedonian language">ancient Macedonian</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[27]</a></sup> which most scholars suggest may have been a <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_dialects" title="Ancient Greek dialects">dialect of Greek</a> itself,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Crespo2018_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-Crespo2018-31">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hatzopoulos2018_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hatzopoulos2018-32">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[30]</a></sup> but it is poorly attested and it is difficult to conclude. Independently of the Macedonian question, some scholars have grouped Greek into <a href="/wiki/Graeco-Phrygian" title="Graeco-Phrygian">Graeco-Phrygian</a>, as Greek and the extinct <a href="/wiki/Phrygian_language" title="Phrygian language">Phrygian</a> share features that are not found in other Indo-European languages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[31]</a></sup> Among living languages, some Indo-Europeanists suggest that Greek may be most closely related to <a href="/wiki/Armenian_language" title="Armenian language">Armenian</a> (see <a href="/wiki/Graeco-Armenian" title="Graeco-Armenian">Graeco-Armenian</a>) or the <a href="/wiki/Indo-Iranian_languages" title="Indo-Iranian languages">Indo-Iranian languages</a> (see <a href="/wiki/Graeco-Aryan" title="Graeco-Aryan">Graeco-Aryan</a>), but little definitive evidence has been found for grouping the living branches of the family.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[32]</a></sup> In addition, <a href="/wiki/Albanian_language" title="Albanian language">Albanian</a> has also been considered somewhat related to Greek and Armenian by some linguists. If proven and recognized, the three languages would form a new Balkan sub-branch with other dead European languages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[33]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Writing_system">Writing system</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Greek_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Writing system">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Linear_B">Linear B</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Greek_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Linear B">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Linear_B" title="Linear B">Linear B</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Linear_B" title="Linear B">Linear B</a>, attested as early as the late 15th century BC, was the first script used to write Greek.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-37">[34]</a></sup> It is basically a <a href="/wiki/Syllabary" title="Syllabary">syllabary</a>, which was finally deciphered by <a href="/wiki/Michael_Ventris" title="Michael Ventris">Michael Ventris</a> and <a href="/wiki/John_Chadwick" title="John Chadwick">John Chadwick</a> in the 1950s (its precursor, <a href="/wiki/Linear_A" title="Linear A">Linear A</a>, has not been deciphered and most likely encodes a non-Greek language).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_37-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-37">[34]</a></sup> The language of the Linear B texts, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mycenaean_Greek_language" title="Mycenaean Greek language">Mycenaean Greek</a>, is the earliest known form of Greek.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_37-2"><a href="#cite_note-:0-37">[34]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cypriot_syllabary">Cypriot syllabary</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Greek_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Cypriot syllabary">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Cypriot_syllabary" title="Cypriot syllabary">Cypriot syllabary</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cypriot_syllabic_inscription_600-500BC.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="422" decoding="async" height="417" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Cypriot_syllabic_inscription_600-500BC.jpg/220px-Cypriot_syllabic_inscription_600-500BC.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Cypriot_syllabic_inscription_600-500BC.jpg/330px-Cypriot_syllabic_inscription_600-500BC.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/ee/Cypriot_syllabic_inscription_600-500BC.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cypriot_syllabic_inscription_600-500BC.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Greek inscription in Cypriot syllabic script</div></div></div>
<p>Another similar system used to write the Greek language was the <a href="/wiki/Cypriot_syllabary" title="Cypriot syllabary">Cypriot syllabary</a> (also a descendant of Linear A via the intermediate <a href="/wiki/Cypro-Minoan_syllabary" title="Cypro-Minoan syllabary">Cypro-Minoan syllabary</a>), which is closely related to Linear B but uses somewhat different syllabic conventions to represent phoneme sequences. The Cypriot syllabary is attested in Cyprus from the 11th century BC until its gradual abandonment in the late Classical period, in favor of the standard Greek alphabet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[35]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Greek_alphabet">Greek alphabet</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Greek_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Greek alphabet">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Greek_alphabet" title="Greek alphabet">Greek alphabet</a> and <a href="/wiki/Greek_orthography" title="Greek orthography">Greek orthography</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Greek_alphabet_variants.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="960" data-file-width="495" decoding="async" height="427" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Greek_alphabet_variants.png/220px-Greek_alphabet_variants.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Greek_alphabet_variants.png/330px-Greek_alphabet_variants.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Greek_alphabet_variants.png/440px-Greek_alphabet_variants.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Greek_alphabet_variants.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Ancient epichoric variants of the Greek alphabet from <a href="/wiki/Euboea" title="Euboea">Euboea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ionia" title="Ionia">Ionia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Athens" title="Athens">Athens</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Corinth" title="Ancient Corinth">Corinth</a> comparing to modern Greek</div></div></div>
<p>Greek has been written in the Greek alphabet since approximately the 9th century BC. It was created by modifying the <a href="/wiki/Phoenician_alphabet" title="Phoenician alphabet">Phoenician alphabet</a>, with the innovation of adopting certain letters to represent the vowels. The variant of the alphabet in use today is essentially the late <a href="/wiki/Ionic_Greek" title="Ionic Greek">Ionic</a> variant, introduced for writing classical <a href="/wiki/Attic_Greek" title="Attic Greek">Attic</a> in 403 BC. In classical Greek, as in classical Latin, only upper-case letters existed. The lower-case Greek letters were developed much later by medieval scribes to permit a faster, more convenient cursive writing style with the use of <a href="/wiki/Ink" title="Ink">ink</a> and <a href="/wiki/Quill" title="Quill">quill</a>.
</p><p>The Greek alphabet consists of 24 letters, each with an uppercase (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Capital_letter" title="Capital letter">majuscule</a>) and lowercase (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lower_case" title="Lower case">minuscule</a>) form. The letter <a href="/wiki/Sigma" title="Sigma">sigma</a> has an additional lowercase form (ς) used in the final position of a word:
</p>

<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Diacritics">Diacritics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Greek_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Diacritics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Greek_diacritics" title="Greek diacritics">Greek diacritics</a></div>
<p>In addition to the letters, the Greek alphabet features a number of <a href="/wiki/Diacritic" title="Diacritic">diacritical signs</a>: three different accent marks (<a href="/wiki/Acute_accent" title="Acute accent">acute</a>, <a href="/wiki/Grave_accent" title="Grave accent">grave</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Circumflex" title="Circumflex">circumflex</a>), originally denoting different shapes of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pitch_accent" title="Pitch accent">pitch accent</a> on the stressed vowel; the so-called breathing marks (<a href="/wiki/Rough_breathing" title="Rough breathing">rough</a> and <a href="/wiki/Smooth_breathing" title="Smooth breathing">smooth breathing</a>), originally used to signal presence or absence of word-initial /h/; and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trema_(diacritic)" title="Trema (diacritic)">diaeresis</a>, used to mark the full syllabic value of a vowel that would otherwise be read as part of a diphthong. These marks were introduced during the course of the Hellenistic period. Actual usage of the grave in <a href="/wiki/Penmanship" title="Penmanship">handwriting</a> saw a rapid decline in favor of uniform usage of the acute during the late 20th century, and it has only been retained in <a href="/wiki/Typography" title="Typography">typography</a>.
</p><p>After the writing reform of 1982, most diacritics are no longer used. Since then, Greek has been written mostly in the simplified <a href="/wiki/Greek_diacritics" title="Greek diacritics">monotonic orthography</a> (or monotonic system), which employs only the acute accent and the diaeresis. The traditional system, now called the polytonic orthography (or polytonic system), is still used internationally for the writing of <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek" title="Ancient Greek">Ancient Greek</a>.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Punctuation">Punctuation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Greek_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Punctuation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In Greek, the question mark is written as the English semicolon, while the functions of the colon and semicolon are performed by a raised point (•), known as the <i><a href="/wiki/Interpunct#Ano_teleia" title="Interpunct">ano teleia</a></i> (<span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><span lang="grc">άνω τελεία</span></span>). In Greek the <a href="/wiki/Comma" title="Comma">comma</a> also functions as a <a href="/wiki/Silent_letter" title="Silent letter">silent letter</a> in a handful of Greek words, principally distinguishing <span title="Greek-language text"><span lang="el"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%CF%8C,%CF%84%CE%B9" title="wikt:ό,τι">ό,τι</a></span></span> (<i>ó,ti</i>, 'whatever') from <span title="Greek-language text"><span lang="el"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%CF%8C%CF%84%CE%B9" title="wikt:ότι">ότι</a></span></span> (<i>óti</i>, 'that').<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[36]</a></sup>
</p><p>Ancient Greek texts often used <i>scriptio continua</i> ('continuous writing'), which means that ancient authors and scribes would write word after word with no spaces or punctuation between words to differentiate or mark boundaries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[37]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Boustrophedon" title="Boustrophedon">Boustrophedon</a>, or bi-directional text, was also used in Ancient Greek.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Latin_alphabet">Latin alphabet</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Greek_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Latin alphabet">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Greek has occasionally been written in the <a href="/wiki/Latin_script" title="Latin script">Latin script</a>, especially in areas under <a href="/wiki/Stato_da_M%C3%A0r" title="Stato da Màr">Venetian rule</a> or by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_Catholicism_in_Greece" title="Roman Catholicism in Greece">Greek Catholics</a>. The term <span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><i lang="grc-Latn">Frankolevantinika</i></span> / <span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><span lang="grc">Φραγκολεβαντίνικα</span></span> applies when the Latin script is used to write Greek in the cultural ambit of Catholicism (because <span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><i lang="grc-Latn">Frankos</i></span> / <span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><span lang="grc">Φράγκος</span></span> is an older Greek term for West-European dating to when most of (Roman Catholic Christian) West Europe was under the control of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Frankish_Empire" title="Frankish Empire">Frankish Empire</a>). <span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><i lang="grc-Latn">Frankochiotika</i></span> / <span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><span lang="grc">Φραγκοχιώτικα</span></span> (meaning 'Catholic Chiot') alludes to the significant presence of Catholic missionaries based on the island of <a href="/wiki/Chios" title="Chios">Chios</a>. Additionally, the term <a href="/wiki/Greeklish" title="Greeklish">Greeklish</a> is often used when the Greek language is written in a Latin script in online communications.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[38]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Latin script is nowadays used by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Griko_dialect" title="Griko dialect">Greek-speaking</a> communities of <a href="/wiki/Southern_Italy" title="Southern Italy">Southern Italy</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hebrew_alphabet">Hebrew alphabet</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Greek_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Hebrew alphabet">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yevanic" title="Yevanic">Yevanic</a> dialect was written by <a href="/wiki/Romaniote_Jews" title="Romaniote Jews">Romaniote</a> and Constantinopolitan Karaite Jews using the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hebrew_Alphabet" title="Hebrew Alphabet">Hebrew Alphabet</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[39]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Arabic_alphabet">Arabic alphabet</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Greek_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Arabic alphabet">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Some <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greek_Muslim" title="Greek Muslim">Greek Muslims</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cretan_Muslim" title="Cretan Muslim">from Crete</a> wrote their <a href="/wiki/Cretan_Greek" title="Cretan Greek">Cretan Greek</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Arabic_alphabet" title="Arabic alphabet">Arabic alphabet</a>. The same happened among Epirote Muslims in <a href="/wiki/Ioannina" title="Ioannina">Ioannina</a>. This usage is sometimes called <a href="/wiki/Aljamiado" title="Aljamiado">aljamiado</a>, as when <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Romance_language" title="Romance language">Romance languages</a> are written in the Arabic alphabet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kotzageorgis_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kotzageorgis-43">[40]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Example_text">Example text</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Greek_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Example text">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Article 1 of the <i><a href="/wiki/Universal_Declaration_of_Human_Rights" title="Universal Declaration of Human Rights">Universal Declaration of Human Rights</a></i> in Greek:
</p>
<dl><dd><span title="Greek-language text"><span lang="el">Όλοι οι άνθρωποι γεννιούνται ελεύθεροι και ίσοι στην αξιοπρέπεια και τα δικαιώματα. Είναι προικισμένοι με λογική και συνείδηση, και οφείλουν να συμπεριφέρονται μεταξύ τους με πνεύμα αδελφοσύνης.</span></span><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[41]</a></sup></dd></dl>
<p><a href="/wiki/Romanization_of_Greek" title="Romanization of Greek">Transcription</a> of the example text into <a href="/wiki/Latin_alphabet" title="Latin alphabet">Latin alphabet</a>:
</p>
<dl><dd><span title="Greek-language romanization"><i lang="el-Latn">Óloi oi ánthropoi gennioúntai eléftheroi kai ísoi stin axioprépeia kai ta dikaiómata. Eínai proikisménoi me logikí kai syneídisi, kai ofeíloun na symperiférontai metaxý tous me pnévma adelfosýnis.</i></span></dd></dl>
<p>Article 1 of the <i>Universal Declaration of Human Rights</i> in English:
</p>
<dl><dd>"All human beings are born free and equal in dignity and rights. They are endowed with reason and conscience and should act towards one another in a spirit of brotherhood."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[42]</a></sup></dd></dl>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Greek_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1011085734">.mw-parser-output .reflist{font-size:90%;margin-bottom:0.5em;list-style-type:decimal}.mw-parser-output .reflist .references{font-size:100%;margin-bottom:0;list-style-type:inherit}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-2{column-width:30em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-3{column-width:25em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns{margin-top:0.3em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns ol{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns li{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-alpha{list-style-type:upper-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-roman{list-style-type:upper-roman}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-alpha{list-style-type:lower-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-greek{list-style-type:lower-greek}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-roman{list-style-type:lower-roman}</style><div class="reflist">
<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-12"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-12">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">A comprehensive overview in J.T. Hooker's <i>Mycenaean Greece</i> (<a href="#CITEREFHooker1976">Hooker 1976</a>, Chapter 2: "Before the Mycenaean Age", pp. 11–33 and passim); for a different hypothesis excluding massive migrations and favoring an autochthonous scenario, see Colin Renfrew's "Problems in the General Correlation of Archaeological and Linguistic Strata in Prehistoric Greece: The Model of Autochthonous Origin" (<a href="#CITEREFRenfrew1973">Renfrew 1973</a>, pp. 263–276, especially p. 267) in <i>Bronze Age Migrations</i> by R.A. Crossland and A. Birchall, eds. (1973).</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-25"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-25">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The four cases that are found in all stages of Greek are the nominative, genitive, accusative, and vocative. The dative/locative of Ancient Greek disappeared in the late Hellenistic period, and the instrumental case of Mycenaean Greek disappeared in the Archaic period.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-26"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-26">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">There is no particular morphological form that can be identified as 'subjunctive' in the modern language, but the term is sometimes encountered in descriptions even if the most complete modern grammar (Holton et al. 1997) does not use it and calls certain traditionally-'subjunctive' forms 'dependent'. Most Greek linguists advocate abandoning the traditional terminology (Anna Roussou and Tasos Tsangalidis 2009, in <i>Meletes gia tin Elliniki Glossa</i>, Thessaloniki, Anastasia Giannakidou 2009 "Temporal semantics and polarity: The dependency of the subjunctive revisited", Lingua); see <a href="/wiki/Modern_Greek_grammar#The_verb" title="Modern Greek grammar">Modern Greek grammar</a> for explanation.</span>
</li>
</ol></div></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Greek_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: References">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Citations">Citations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Greek_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Citations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1011085734" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="reflist">
<div class="mw-references-wrap mw-references-columns"><ol class="references">

<li id="cite_note-42"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-42">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation web cs1"><a class="external text" href="https://www.omniglot.com/writing/yevanic.htm" rel="nofollow">"Yevanic alphabet, pronunciation and language"</a>. <i>www.omniglot.com</i><span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">18 April</span> 2020</span>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.jtitle=www.omniglot.com&amp;rft.atitle=Yevanic+alphabet%2C+pronunciation+and+language&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.omniglot.com%2Fwriting%2Fyevanic.htm&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AGreek+language"></span></span>

<li id="cite_note-Kotzageorgis-43"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Kotzageorgis_43-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFKotzageorgis2010">Kotzageorgis, Phokion (2010).  Gruber, Christiane J.; Colby, Frederick Stephen (eds.). <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=sjLHirJmvPUC&amp;q=Reworking+the+Ascension+in+Ottoman+Lands:+An+Eighteenth-Century+Mi%27r%C4%81jn%C4%81ma+in+Greek+from+Epirus&amp;pg=PA297" rel="nofollow"><i>The Prophet's Ascension: Cross-cultural Encounters with the Islamic Mi'rāj Tales</i></a>. Indiana University Press. p. 297. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-253-35361-0" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-253-35361-0"><bdi>978-0-253-35361-0</bdi></a>. <q>The element that makes this text a <i><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/unicum" title="wiktionary:unicum">unicum</a></i> is that it is written in Greek script. In the Ottoman Empire, the primary criterion for the selection of an alphabet in which to write was religion. Thus, people who did not speak—or even know—the official language of their religion used to write their religious texts in the languages that they knew, though in the alphabet where the sacred texts of that religion were written. Thus, the Grecophone Catholics of Chios wrote using the Latin alphabet, but in the Greek language (<i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Frangochiotika&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Frangochiotika (page does not exist)">frangochiotika</a></i>); the Turcophone Orthodox Christians of Cappadocia wrote their Turkish texts using the Greek alphabet (<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Karamanlidika" title="Karamanlidika">karamanlidika</a></i>); and the Grecophone Muslims of the Greek peninsula wrote in Greek language using the Arabic alphabet (<i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Tourkogianniotika&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Tourkogianniotika (page does not exist)">tourkogianniotika</a></i>, <i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Tourkokretika&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Tourkokretika (page does not exist)">tourkokretika</a></i>). Our case is much stranger, since it is a quite early example for that kind of literature and because it is largely concerned with religious themes."; p. 306. The audience for the Greek <i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Mi%27r%C4%81jn%C4%81ma&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Mi'rājnāma (page does not exist)">Mi'rājnāma</a></i> was most certainly Greek-speaking Muslims, in particular the so-called <i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Tourkogianniot&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Tourkogianniot (page does not exist)">Tourkogianniotes</a></i> (literally, the Turks of Jannina). Although few examples have been discovered as yet, it seems that these people developed a religious literature mainly composed in verse form. This literary form constituted the mainstream of Greek <i><a href="/wiki/Aljamiado" title="Aljamiado">Aljamiado</a></i> literature from the middle of the seventeenth century until the <a href="/wiki/Population_exchange_between_Greece_and_Turkey" title="Population exchange between Greece and Turkey">population exchange between Greece and Turkey</a> in 1923. Tourkogianniotes were probably of Christian origin and were Islamized sometime during the seventeenth century. They did not speak any language other than Greek. Thus, even their frequency in attending mosque services did not provide them with the necessary knowledge about their faith. Given their low level of literacy, one important way that they could learn about their faith was to listen to religiously edifying texts such as the Greek <i>Mi'rājnāma</i>.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=The+Prophet%27s+Ascension%3A+Cross-cultural+Encounters+with+the+Islamic+Mi%27r%C4%81j+Tales&amp;rft.pages=297&amp;rft.pub=Indiana+University+Press&amp;rft.date=2010&amp;rft.isbn=978-0-253-35361-0&amp;rft.aulast=Kotzageorgis&amp;rft.aufirst=Phokion&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3DsjLHirJmvPUC%26q%3DReworking%2Bthe%2BAscension%2Bin%2BOttoman%2BLands%3A%2BAn%2BEighteenth-Century%2BMi%2527r%25C4%2581jn%25C4%2581ma%2Bin%2BGreek%2Bfrom%2BEpirus%26pg%3DPA297&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AGreek+language"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-44"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-44">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation web cs1"><a class="external text" href="https://www.ohchr.org/en/udhr/pages/Language.aspx?LangID=grk" rel="nofollow">"Universal Declaration of Human Rights"</a>. <i>ohchr.org</i>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.jtitle=ohchr.org&amp;rft.atitle=Universal+Declaration+of+Human+Rights&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.ohchr.org%2Fen%2Fudhr%2Fpages%2FLanguage.aspx%3FLangID%3Dgrk&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AGreek+language"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-45"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-45">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation news cs1"><a class="external text" href="https://www.un.org/en/about-us/universal-declaration-of-human-rights" rel="nofollow">"Universal Declaration of Human Rights"</a>. <i>United Nations</i>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=United+Nations&amp;rft.atitle=Universal+Declaration+of+Human+Rights&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.un.org%2Fen%2Fabout-us%2Funiversal-declaration-of-human-rights&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AGreek+language"></span></span>

<dl><dt>General background</dt></dl>
<ul><li><a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20051013090119/http://www.bartleby.com/65/gr/Greeklan.html" rel="nofollow">Greek Language</a>, Columbia Electronic Encyclopedia.</li>
<li><a class="external text" href="http://greeklinguistics.com" rel="nofollow">The Greek Language and Linguistics Gateway</a>, useful information on the history of the Greek language, application of modern Linguistics to the study of Greek, and tools for learning Greek.</li>
<li>Aristotle University of Thessaloniki, <a class="external text" href="http://www.greek-language.gr/greekLang/index.html" rel="nofollow">The Greek Language Portal</a>, a portal for Greek language and linguistic education.</li>
<li><a class="external text" href="https://www.perseus.tufts.edu/" rel="nofollow">The Perseus Project</a> has many useful pages for the study of classical languages and literatures, including dictionaries.</li>
<li><a class="external text" href="http://ucbclassics.dreamhosters.com/ancgreek/ancient_greek_start.html" rel="nofollow">Ancient Greek Tutorials</a>, Berkeley Language Center of the University of California, Berkeley</li></ul>
<dl><dt>Language learning</dt></dl>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1097025294" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="side-box side-box-right plainlinks sistersitebox">
<div class="side-box-flex">
<div class="side-box-image"><img alt="" class="noviewer" data-file-height="355" data-file-width="300" decoding="async" height="40" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Wikiquote-logo.svg/34px-Wikiquote-logo.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Wikiquote-logo.svg/51px-Wikiquote-logo.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Wikiquote-logo.svg/68px-Wikiquote-logo.svg.png 2x" width="34"/></div>

</div>
<ul><li><a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20171222162856/https://greeklinguistics.com/grammar/" rel="nofollow">Hellenistic Greek Lessons</a> Greek-Language.com provides a free online grammar of Hellenistic Greek.</li>
<li><a class="external text" href="http://www.komvos.edu.gr/" rel="nofollow">komvos.edu.gr</a>, a website for the support of people who are being taught the Greek language.</li>
<li><a class="external text" href="http://www.ntgreek.net/" rel="nofollow">New Testament Greek</a> Three graduated courses designed to help students learn to read the Greek New Testament</li>
<li><a class="external text" href="http://www.pi-schools.gr/lessons/hellenic/" rel="nofollow">Books on Greek language that are taught at schools in Greece (page in Greek)</a></li>

<li><a class="external text" href="https://www.livelingua.com/fsi-greek-course.php" rel="nofollow">USA Foreign Service Institute Modern Greek basic course</a></li>
<li><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation web cs1" id="CITEREFAversa">Aversa, Alan. <a class="external text" href="http://www.u.arizona.edu/~aversa/greek/" rel="nofollow">"Greek Inflector"</a>. University of Arizona.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.btitle=Greek+Inflector&amp;rft.pub=University+of+Arizona&amp;rft.aulast=Aversa&amp;rft.aufirst=Alan&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.u.arizona.edu%2F~aversa%2Fgreek%2F&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AGreek+language"></span> Identifies the grammatical functions of all the words in sentences entered, using Perseus.</li></ul>
<dl><dt>Dictionaries</dt></dl>
<ul><li><a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20190711164623/https://www.greeklinguistics.com/Dictionaries.html" rel="nofollow">Greek Lexical Aids</a>, descriptions of both online lexicons (with appropriate links) and Greek Lexicons in Print.</li>
<li><a class="external text" href="http://www.greek-language.gr/greekLang/index.html" rel="nofollow">The Greek Language Portal</a>, dictionaries of all forms of Greek (Ancient, Hellenistic, Medieval, Modern)</li>

<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Proto-Greek_language" title="Proto-Greek language">Proto-Greek</a> - (c. 2900–1600 BC)</li>

<li><a href="/wiki/Koine_Greek" title="Koine Greek">Koine Greek</a> - (c. 300 BC–AD 330)</li>
